    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2160685565" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2160685565" />
    <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova"><img alt="Jeannette Nikolova" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480677087p2/2574284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2160685565?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeannette Nikolova" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeannette Nikolova" href="/user/show/2574284-jeannette-nikolova">Jeannette Nikolova</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2160685565" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6665262747536846526"><a href='https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2017/12/23/the-joke-by-milan-kundera/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br/><br/>One of my all-time favourite books is Kundera's <strong>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</strong>. So when I went to the Czech republic, I wanted to get a new Kundera book along my plan of buying books from the countries I visit. I had heard about <strong>The Joke</strong> from several people, so I told myself "Why not?". Well... I shouldn't have.<br/><br/>This book represented everything I could possibly hate about Kundera. I had heard before that he has many misogynistic tones in his books, </span>
  <span id="freeText6665262747536846526" style="display:none"><a href="https://wondrousbooks.wordpress.com/2017/12/23/the-joke-by-milan-kundera/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Also available on the WondrousBooks blog.</i>
</a><br /><br />One of my all-time favourite books is Kundera's <strong>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</strong>. So when I went to the Czech republic, I wanted to get a new Kundera book along my plan of buying books from the countries I visit. I had heard about <strong>The Joke</strong> from several people, so I told myself "Why not?". Well... I shouldn't have.<br /><br />This book represented everything I could possibly hate about Kundera. I had heard before that he has many misogynistic tones in his books, something that didn't strike me as hard in <strong>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</strong>, but it did so in <strong>The Joke</strong>. In fact, I had an extremely hard time finishing this book because of the terrible representation of human emotions, interpersonal feelings and the role of women in them.<br /><br />In <strong>The Joke</strong>, women are nothing more than playthings. The only thing they do is<em> serve a purpose</em>. They are not beings on their own but only in relation to what men need from them. The characters themselves admit it at certain points, but it was <strong>not</strong> my impression that while the author does self-mock, he also criticizes this open misogyny. I don't think that Kundera actually disagrees with his characters. He might realize that the roles he attributes to women are wrong, but I don't think he has any other way of thinking, and that is clearly visible in the entire book.<br /><br />This fact bothered me so deeply that I could focus on little else outside of it.<br /><br />Throughout the entire book, I was deeply disturbed and disgusted by the fact that this is how some men see and treat women. This indifference, this humiliation, it was so scary - if we are to accept the world as seen through Kundera, it would be a sad world indeed. And the most terrifying part of it all is that this type of behaviour is not only real, but also very common. I noticed even more of the exactly same attitude toward women, while I was reading the book, in the world around me.<br /><br /><em>Indifference</em> was also what was killing the characters in the book. Helena was tortured by the indifference of her lovers, Ludvik - by the indifference of the other people to his sorrows and need for revenge, Jaroslav - by the indifference of the modern world to his beloved traditions and folklore.<br /><br />I believe that we all shudder at the idea of indifference. Anger is passion, same as love. It means that a person cares, one way or another. But indifference... that is altogether different and scarier. It means that to someone, or to a group of people, or even to the whole world, something that you care about, or worse yet, your entire being, is something of no importance and no consequence. And there is nothing at all to do to fight indifference. A cold and indifferent heart can hardly be shaken by any desperate action.<br /><br />There was one character that I found more tolerable than the entire bunch - Kostka. He was the only character that was not entirely closed off into his own world and wanted to give and not just get. There was also one quote from Kostka that made me think long and hard until I ended up agreeing:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <em>"I can understand you, but that doesn't alter the fact that such general rancor against people is terrifying and sinful. <strong>Because to live in a world in which no one is forgiven, where all are irredeemable , is the same as living in hell.</strong> You are living in hell, Ludvik, and I pity you."</em>
  <br />
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="6665262747536846526" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_475091006" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/475091006" />
    <a title="Dan Keating" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5059274-dan-keating"><img alt="Dan Keating" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299018039p2/5059274.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475091006?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Keating" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Keating" href="/user/show/5059274-dan-keating">Dan Keating</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475091006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13904923046848710714">In "The Joke" we have vintage Kundera - emotionally and intellectually challenging, ripe with the intertwining symbolism of the banal and the mystical, filled to the brim with romance, tragedy, comedy, and a pastiche of kitsch, which never once falls into kitsch itself. Invoking a bit of irony, the novel is quite simply "marvelous."<br/><br/>"The Joke" tells the story of several characters at various points in a more-or-less fifteen year period, but is principally the tale of Ludvik Jahn, a fallen Czech C</span>
  <span id="freeText13904923046848710714" style="display:none">In "The Joke" we have vintage Kundera - emotionally and intellectually challenging, ripe with the intertwining symbolism of the banal and the mystical, filled to the brim with romance, tragedy, comedy, and a pastiche of kitsch, which never once falls into kitsch itself. Invoking a bit of irony, the novel is quite simply "marvelous."<br /><br />"The Joke" tells the story of several characters at various points in a more-or-less fifteen year period, but is principally the tale of Ludvik Jahn, a fallen Czech Communist whose attempts at both sentimentality and cynicism are both crashing failures, principally due to his inability to see anything in others beyond his use for them. While Ludvig himself condemns himself for this flaw, it is revealed as a greater flaw of humanity; even those who see more of the people around them and are less selfish ultimately realize that they neither know themselves nor the objects of their affections; their desires for happiness are underlined by vague imperatives which are shaken and ultimately cast aside by the realities of the people around them.<br /><br />Stylistically, those who have read Kundera's masterpiece "The Unbearable Lightness of Being" will likely find "The Joke" a more accessible read. That said, those unfamiliar with Kundera at all will likely be thrown by his highly non-linear style. Kundera uses first-person narration throughout the story, often switching narrators with no clear identification up front as to who is speaking. While these narrations proceed in a mostly straightforward fashion in terms of chronology, the heavy emphasis on reminiscence and the prevalence of retelling of memories makes the book feel extremely non-linear. This will likely sour many readers; I can only say that your perseverance will be rewarded if you tough out the first couple of perspective/time shifts.<br /><br />In terms of pure diction, we see in "The Joke" a much more lyrically playful Kundera; the novel's text is rife with poetry and even includes musical notation in one section. Aside from these overt examples, though, the actual narration itself has a very lyrical flavor. Ludvig is as classically Eastern European as there is in fiction; everything from his advanced vocabulary to his predilection for advancing from aloof to an emotional geyser at the drop of a hat is classically European, with (deeply ironic) shades of the Russian masters Nabokov and Dostoevsky. His extreme counterpoint, Helena, is given long statements punctuated by commas, revealing a rambling mentality which never quite comes to completion. Each character has a relatively unique voice, and it is really only when a new narrator takes the stage that confusion occurs.<br /><br />The novel takes a toll on readers, both emotionally and intellectually. The constant farce that is Ludvig's existence only ever seems to get worse. He is unarguably responsible for this at several junctures, and what's worse is that he's always aware on some level of this. Ludvig is a massively detail-oriented character, and unfortunately, to himself, his life has been defined by the details he missed, not those that he observed and carefully attempted to twist to his liking. It is left to the reader to decide which is Ludvig's greatest tragedy; there are more than I care to share here, but suffice to say that by the end of the novel it would be difficult to make a full accounting for his misery.<br /><br />It bears mentioning that "The Joke" will likely upset people who are offended by reading about abuse directed toward women. Indeed, Kundera does not present us with a single solidly strong female character, and while Ludvig's realization that his own treatment of women (or just Lucie in particular) as objects has led to his misery (and is therefore presented in a negative light), there is little thereafter that addresses the issue directly. <br /><br />That said, I still highly recommend "The Joke" to anyone willing to put the time and effort into it. Definitely give this one a shot if you're able. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13904923046848710714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2770840736" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2770840736" />
    <a title="Padmaja (thebookishtales)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63656912-padmaja-thebookishtales"><img alt="Padmaja (thebookishtales)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603572746p2/63656912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2770840736?book_show_action=true">Jun 26, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Padmaja (thebookishtales)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Padmaja (thebookishtales)" href="/user/show/63656912-padmaja-thebookishtales">Padmaja (thebookishtales)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780060995058" href="/book/show/397281.The_Joke">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2770840736" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8111136438007507449">The joke, originally published in Czech in 1967 as The Zert, is Kundera's first novel.<br/>As it's title, the book is full of many jokes. There are seven chapters in the book and the story is told from the POV's of different characters. The timeline oscillates back and forth. I was really impressed by the depth of this book, and the eclectic characters.<br/>Each character throws different light on the same events each character describes previously.<br/>~<br/>It's the Czechoslovakia of the 1950's, Ludvik Jahn, our </span>
  <span id="freeText8111136438007507449" style="display:none">The joke, originally published in Czech in 1967 as The Zert, is Kundera's first novel.<br />As it's title, the book is full of many jokes. There are seven chapters in the book and the story is told from the POV's of different characters. The timeline oscillates back and forth. I was really impressed by the depth of this book, and the eclectic characters.<br />Each character throws different light on the same events each character describes previously.<br />~<br />It's the Czechoslovakia of the 1950's, Ludvik Jahn, our protagonist, is a strong supporter of the Communist regime after World War 2. In order to charm his girlfriend, he writes a joke on a postcard which backfired on the Commies. He is expelled from the party and is sent for military service. He harbours a grudge against the party members, especially against Zemanik, who had led the charge against him. After many years, Ludvik seduces his wife and now the 'Joke' is on Zemanik.<br />Lucie, Ludvik's girlfriend for a brief period of time, never gets to tell her story. I really wish Lucie had a separate chapter as well. I would've loved it to hear in her voice instead of a man who narrated her story. She was just there as a piece of female flesh in the book. I particularly connected with Jaroslav, who mourns the loss of respect towards folk music and songs. His was a very likeable character.<br />~<br />To sum up my feelings, I felt it was a nice commentary on the political situation of that time. It heavily messed up with my head. I have no inclination towards communism, and some of the theories went over my head. It took some time to sink in and the narrative takes some time to get used to. The plot overlaps, because the story is told by different characters. I was particularly unhappy towards Ludvik's act of revenge.<br />All in all, this book made for a compelling read. It was laborious to get into, but it was a good read. I didn't enjoy this one as much as I enjoyed The Unbearable lightness of being. I am not willing to say goodbye to Mr Kundera yet. I will explore more of his words and then decide for myself.<br />3.5⭐</span>
  <a data-text-id="8111136438007507449" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_803971018" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/803971018" />
    <a title="Jibran" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran"><img alt="Jibran" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572721231p2/5407952.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/803971018?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jibran" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jibran" href="/user/show/5407952-jibran">Jibran</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5407952-jibran?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer803971018" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14126258066796595788">I read it as part of a set of debut novels by novelists who later became major names in contemporary literature. This novel is written in Czech language and later translated into English as a group effort which included the novelist himself. I have been a fan of Kundera since I read his insightful expositions on the art of novel and on the nature of art in general. <br/><br/>The rise and consolidation of Communist rule in 1950s Czechoslovakia forms the background of this novel. The protagonist, himself a </span>
  <span id="freeText14126258066796595788" style="display:none">I read it as part of a set of debut novels by novelists who later became major names in contemporary literature. This novel is written in Czech language and later translated into English as a group effort which included the novelist himself. I have been a fan of Kundera since I read his insightful expositions on the art of novel and on the nature of art in general. <br /><br />The rise and consolidation of Communist rule in 1950s Czechoslovakia forms the background of this novel. The protagonist, himself a budding Communist student, is expunged from the party and kicked out of the university to be sent to slave away in the mines run by the military. Why? He unwittingly made a joke, in a letter to his girlfriend, about the Communist politics and leaders. Her dedicated and mirthless girlfriend took it to be a sacrilege - a blasphemy of religious proportions - and reported it to the authorities. <br /><br />The story revolves around his attempts to come to terms with the turn of his fate as he languishes in a military camp in a battalian which is supposed to comprise of the most dangerous anti-state opposition figures. But he finds out that most dissenters had been put their for petty reasons: for not supporting wholeheartedly and enthusiastically one of the policies of the Communist government; and another is punished simply as a preemptive measure because of his father who's a oppositional activist. <br /><br />When he regains limited freedom after a few years of hard labour at the military camp, writing in hate for what his life has become, he launches himself on a campaign to take revenge on another of his friends who had him voted out of the university and the party and who in effect authored his subsequent travails. This is where I think the novel becomes thin. His personal philosophy suffers a painful realisation of some sort and makes him do something he wasn't planning.<br /><br />It's a good debut novel in that it goes beyond the usual noisy political sloganeering found in novels that deal with highly charged political subject matter, such as Orwell's 1984, and delves deeper into the mysteries of totalitarian political collective on a psychological level, but unlike anything resembling the oft-seen 'pop psychology' which caricatures the problem and which any average person can write about. Jokes - humour and sarcasm - contends Kundera, is the most mysteriously powerful of dissenting tools in the world, and any power - be it religious, political, moral, etc - which has taken upon itself to reform the whole world in earnest, is greatly threatened by it, and can't tolerate it.<br /><br />In the case of the hero of our novel, the Communist government elevated a pathetic half-serious joke to the same status as of dangerous anti-state activity when, at the military camp, the authorities assigned him to a battalion reserved for top level dissenters - and this for us is the real joke.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14126258066796595788" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer23326573" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4619360246296724146">His first novel - and not nearly as good as ULoB. Set in Communist Prague. Different chapters told by different protagonists, so the plot overlaps. Mostly revolving around a man expelled from the Party for an inappropriate joke (apparently supporting Trotsky) and other misunderstandings that blight his life. Vividly, and sadly, portrays the frustrations and hypocrisy of Communist control.</span>

<div id="review_830365356" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/830365356" />
    <a title="Stacia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3917029-stacia"><img alt="Stacia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1316011190p2/3917029.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/830365356?book_show_action=true">Jan 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stacia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stacia" href="/user/show/3917029-stacia">Stacia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780060190309" href="/book/show/1410878.The_Joke">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3917029-stacia?shelf=europe">europe</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer830365356" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12846606779614080993">It always takes me a little while to process books before I can decide how I truly feel about them. The words that pop into mind right after finishing the book are sad, ironic, bittersweet. The regrets of life & looking back, the loss of culture through time. There is also a feel emanating from the book coming from a Communist-era society, the pressures of needing to fit in (vs. standing out), an overall melancholy (amidst the dark, ironic humor) that permeates some eastern European works of tha</span>
  <span id="freeText12846606779614080993" style="display:none">It always takes me a little while to process books before I can decide how I truly feel about them. The words that pop into mind right after finishing the book are sad, ironic, bittersweet. The regrets of life &amp; looking back, the loss of culture through time. There is also a feel emanating from the book coming from a Communist-era society, the pressures of needing to fit in (vs. standing out), an overall melancholy (amidst the dark, ironic humor) that permeates some eastern European works of that time period (Cold War era).<br><br>In a way it seems fitting to have read this novel in the winter as it has a cool, somewhat detached tone to it.<br><br>I enjoyed the descriptions of traditional/old Czech weddings, <a href="http://www.unesco.org/culture/ich/index.php?lg=en&amp;pg=00011&amp;RL=00564" rel="nofollow noopener">'Ride of the Kings' ceremonies</a>, and <a href="http://ostrava.cimbalovamuzika.cz/index_en.htm" rel="nofollow noopener">Czech/Moravian cimbalom bands</a>. The folkloric traditions in the book sound beautiful &amp; wonderful (even if they are tinged with regret in the novel).<br><br>Ride of the Kings:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391143497i/8336059.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Cimbalom band:<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1391143497i/8336060.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>Though the overall tone was bleak &amp; distant, I will rate the book a 4 for masterful &amp; evocative writing (esp. since this was Kundera's first novel).</span>
  <a data-text-id="12846606779614080993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2769554805" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2769554805" />
    <a title="Rhea (Rufus Reads)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23948506-rhea-rufus-reads"><img alt="Rhea (Rufus Reads)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586770629p2/23948506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2769554805?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rhea (Rufus Reads)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rhea (Rufus Reads)" href="/user/show/23948506-rhea-rufus-reads">Rhea (Rufus Reads)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571166930" href="/book/show/43504358-the-joke">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2769554805" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2594857165438473001">Kundera's writing always wins. As much as he wants to maintain that this is 'just a novel', it's surely a political manifesto.<br/><br/>There were some great things in here: set in communist Czech, before the Prague Spring, when the authoritarianism started causing cracks. The philosophical motivations. The three dimensional perspective of events with different character narrations. And the relevance of the book's title as the plot was essentially a joke.<br/><br/>But I couldn't shake off one thing. This book - Kun</span>
  <span id="freeText2594857165438473001" style="display:none">Kundera's writing always wins. As much as he wants to maintain that this is 'just a novel', it's surely a political manifesto.<br /><br />There were some great things in here: set in communist Czech, before the Prague Spring, when the authoritarianism started causing cracks. The philosophical motivations. The three dimensional perspective of events with different character narrations. And the relevance of the book's title as the plot was essentially a joke.<br /><br />But I couldn't shake off one thing. This book - Kundera's first - was written with an over bearing male gaze. Women characters were hyper sexualized and easily discarded. All the women in this book - ALL - were just tools for the men's stories. The use of femininity so problematic. The handling of rape and sexual assault and autonomy were so poorly done. Every fifth page my face cringed and I almost gave the book up.<br /><br />It's hard, to reconcile my admiration for Kundera, as I read this. It's true that Kundera's books have shitty protagonists and he doesn't write them to be liked. But this was sheer misogynistic. And I don't think it can pass off as being historical fiction or simply a protagonist POV. <br /><br />Breaks my heart to give Kundera 2 stars - but I've had enough of books that don't do women justice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2594857165438473001" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Parikhit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4401637-parikhit"><img alt="Parikhit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1518518014p2/4401637.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/397699359?book_show_action=true">Aug 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Parikhit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Parikhit" href="/user/show/4401637-parikhit">Parikhit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4401637-parikhit?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4401637-parikhit?shelf=arm-chair-challenge-2012">arm-chair-challenge-2012</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer397699359" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5002038064704910107">I am disappointed to have ignored Milan Kundera for such a long time but Milan Kundera happened at the right time of my life. Doubts, questions that have been pounding in my head, deception, remorse and nostalgia-I found all that in the book. <br/><br/>No, the book was not the search of an almost recluse to discover parallel thoughts. ‘The Joke’ just happened. Yes there is a plot but the ideas and debates were what made the book effective. Uncanny and cruel be the fact that stray thoughts, innocent musing</span>
  <span id="freeText5002038064704910107" style="display:none">I am disappointed to have ignored Milan Kundera for such a long time but Milan Kundera happened at the right time of my life. Doubts, questions that have been pounding in my head, deception, remorse and nostalgia-I found all that in the book. <br /><br />No, the book was not the search of an almost recluse to discover parallel thoughts. ‘The Joke’ just happened. Yes there is a plot but the ideas and debates were what made the book effective. Uncanny and cruel be the fact that stray thoughts, innocent musings and impetuous utterances can bring about a drastic change to everything we know, believe and stand for. ‘The Joke’ expounds that and much more. Life is but a cruel joke. <br /><br />Milan Kundera has me chained to his thoughts and writing-simple but beautiful. Quoting a couple of lines here.<br />‘I was not a hypocrite, with one real face and several false ones. I had several faces because I was young and didn’t know who I was or wanted to be. (I was frightened by the differences between one face and the next; none of them seemed to fit me properly, and I groped my way clumsily among them.)’<br /><br />‘Yes, suddenly I saw it clearly: most people deceive themselves with a pair of faiths: they believe in eternal memory (of people, things, deeds, nations) and in redressibility (of deeds, mistakes, sins, wrongs). Both are false faiths. In reality the opposite is true: everything will be forgotten and nothing will be redressed. The task of obtaining redress (by vengeance or by forgiveness) will be taken over by forgetting, No one will remember the wrongs that have been done, but all wrongs will be forgotten.’<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5002038064704910107" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8603256292819151070">There were definitely times when I wanted to breakdown about my own life while reading this book. I wanted to shake my fist and curse the powers that be for being cruel tricksters for playing us all in one long painful joke. However, what do you do at the end of the joke? Laugh. This book inspires you want to laugh too and to realize the futility of hanging on to every personal injustice that has been been committed against you; likely you are no better than the perpetrator, and he/she is no wor</span>
  <span id="freeText8603256292819151070" style="display:none">There were definitely times when I wanted to breakdown about my own life while reading this book. I wanted to shake my fist and curse the powers that be for being cruel tricksters for playing us all in one long painful joke. However, what do you do at the end of the joke? Laugh. This book inspires you want to laugh too and to realize the futility of hanging on to every personal injustice that has been been committed against you; likely you are no better than the perpetrator, and he/she is no worse from you.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Mq1SUhsVWI34RidjAZ5b5JkncZMcGFf8H/Uzs5rqtIV2Id1Ouodgqhk7kdiz4PEvCmB/wTbXhHF6OKkLWZCCbg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('H+IOIhAf63CF46ZjYKUq5iT/9VqLHXPNJk2s6ltJ1ElbboE+sY3TV2SeENjS24Att7j7CKHSoEBDgDZSmDPiog==')})
  }

Ditie is called upon to serve not the King of England, but Haile Selassie. It is one of the great moments in his life. Eventually, he falls in love with a Nazi woman athlete as the Germans are invading Czechoslovakia. After the war, through the sale of valuable stamps confiscated from the Jews, he reaches the heights of his ambition, building a hotel. He becomes a millionaire, but with the institution of communism, he loses everything and is sent to inspect mountain roads. Living in dreary circumstances, Ditie comes to terms with the inevitability of his death, and with his place in history.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6278333552843420568\" href=\"#\" May, h<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6034184852724822862\" style=\"display:none\">Often compared to Byron, Keats, Shelley, and Poe, called Lautreamont\'s &quot;elder brother&quot; by the Czech Surrealists, Karel Hynek Mácha (1810-1836) was the greatest Czech Romantic poet, and arguably the most influential of any poet in the language. May, his epic masterpiece, was published in April 1836, just seven months before his death. Considered the &quot;pearl&quot; of Czech poetry, it is a tale of seduction, revenge, and patricide. A paean as well to nature, the beauty of its music and its innovative use of language, expertly captured in this new translation by Marcela Sulak, has ensured the poem\'s lasting popularity. Scorned at first by the national revivalists of the 19th century for being &quot;un-Czech,&quot; Mácha was held up as a &quot;national&quot; poet by later generations, a fate which the interwar Czech avant-garde, who considered him a precursor, took it upon themselves to reverse.\n\nUnlike the other seminal 19th-century European poets, Mácha\'s work has been largely ignored in English translation. The present volume, the only available in English, provides the original Czech text in parallel and includes a series of illustrations by Jindřich Štyrský specifically created for the poem.<\/span>\n <span id="freeTextContainer1472616621107235606">Milan Kundera is a Czech and French writer of Czech origin who has lived in exile in France since 1975, where he became a naturalized French citizen in 1981. He is best known for <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i>, <i>The Book of Laughter and Forgetting</i>, and <i>The Joke</i>.<br/><br/>Kundera has written in both Czech and French. He revises the French translations of all his books; these therefore are not considered tr</span>
  <span id="freeText1472616621107235606" style="display:none">Milan Kundera is a Czech and French writer of Czech origin who has lived in exile in France since 1975, where he became a naturalized French citizen in 1981. He is best known for <i>The Unbearable Lightness of Being</i>, <i>The Book of Laughter and Forgetting</i>, and <i>The Joke</i>.<br /><br />Kundera has written in both Czech and French. He revises the French translations of all his books; these therefore are not considered translations but original works. <br /><br />Due to censorship by the Communist government of Czechoslovakia, his books were banned from his native country, and that remained the case until the downfall of this government in the Velvet Revolution of 1989. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1472616621107235606" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Yes, suddenly I saw it clearly: most people deceive themselves with a pair of faiths: they believe in <i>eternal memory</i> (of people, things, deeds, nations) and in <i>redressibility</i> (of deeds, mistakes, sins, wrongs). Both are false faiths. In reality the opposite is true: everything will be forgotten and nothing will be redressed. The task of obtaining redress (by vengeance or by forgiveness) will be taken over by forgetting. No one will redress the wrongs that have been done, but all wrongs will be forgotten.&rdquo;</span>
